<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Both the dissent and the court below refer to the statement in <em>Cutter </em>v.<em> Wilkinson</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S. 709, 720 (2005), that "courts must take adequate account of the burdens a requested accommodation may impose on nonbeneficiaries," but that statement was made in response to the argument that RFRA's twin, the Religious Land Use and Institutionalized Persons Act, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2000cc <em>et seq.</em>, violated the Establishment Clause.  The only case cited by <em>Cutter</em> in connection with this statement, Estate of Thornton v. Caldor, Inc.,<em>&nbsp;472 U.&nbsp;S. 703 (1985)</em>, involved a religious accommodation that the Court held violated the Establishment Clause.  Before this Court, the States do not argue--and there is no basis for an argument--that the new rule violates that Clause.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The First Amendment cannot have separately justified the church exemption, as the dissent suggests.  See <em>post</em>, at 12-13 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg, J.</small></span>).  That exemption enables a religious institution to decline to provide contraceptive coverage to <em>all</em> its employees, from a minister to a building custodian.  By contrast, the so-called ministerial exception of the First Amendment (which the dissent cites, see <em>post</em>, at 13) extends only to <em>select</em> employees, having ministerial status.  See <em>Our Lady of Guadalupe School </em>v. <em>Morrissey-Berru</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 14-16); <em>Hosanna-Tabor Evangelical Lutheran Church and School</em> v. <em>EEOC</em>, 565 U.&nbsp;S. 171, 190 (2012).  (Too, this Court has applied the ministerial exception only to protect religious institutions from employment discrimination suits, expressly reserving whether the exception excuses their non-compliance with other laws.  See <em>id.</em>, at 196.)  And there is no general constitutional immunity, over and above the ministerial exception, that can protect a religious institution from the law's operation.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I speak here only of the substantive validity of the exemptions.  I agree with the Court that the final rules issuing the exemptions were procedurally valid.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;At oral argument, the Solicitor General argued that the rule's overinclusion is harmless because the accommodation remains available to all employers who qualify for the exemption.  See Tr. of Oral Arg. 20-23.  But in their final rule, the Departments themselves acknowledged the prospect that some employers without a religious objection to the accommodation would switch to the exemption.  See 83 Fed. Reg. 57576-57577 ("Of course, some of the[&nbsp;] religious" institutions that "do not conscientiously oppose participating" in the accommodation "may opt for the expanded exemption[,] but others might not"); <em>id.,</em> at 57561 ("[I]t is not clear to the Departments" how many of the religious employers who had used the accommodation without objection "will choose to use the expanded exemption instead").  And the Solicitor General, when pressed at argument, could offer no evidence that, since the rule took effect, employers without the Little Sisters' complicity beliefs had declined to avail themselves of the new exemption.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 22.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In a brief passage in the interim final rule, the Departments suggested that an exemption is "more workable" than the accommodation in addressing religious objections to the mandate.  82 Fed. Reg. 47806.  But the Departments continue to provide the accommodation to any religious employers who request that option, thus maintaining a two-track system.  See <em>ante,</em> at 10; n.&nbsp;3, <em>supra</em>.  So ease of administration cannot support, at least without more explanation, the Departments' decision to offer the exemption more broadly than needed.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Employment Div.</em>,<em> Dept. of Human Resources of Ore</em>. v. <em>Smith</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 872 (1990), the Court explained that "the right of free exercise does not relieve an individual of the obligation to comply with a valid and neutral law of general applicability on the ground that the law proscribes (or prescribes) conduct that his religion prescribes (or proscribes)."  <em>Id.,</em> at 879 (internal quotation marks omitted).  The requirement that insurers cover FDA-approved methods of contraception "applies generally, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. trains on women's well-being, not on the exercise of religion, and any effect it has on such exercise is incidental."  <em>Burwell</em> v. <em>Hobby Lobby Stores</em>,<em> Inc</em>., 573 U.&nbsp;S. 682, 745 (2014) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, J., dissenting).  <em>Smith </em>forecloses "[a]ny First Amendment Free Exercise Clause claim [one] might assert" in opposition to that requirement.  573 U.&nbsp;S<em>.</em>, at 744.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This requirement does not apply to employers with fewer than 50 employees, 26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 4980H(c)(2), or "grandfathered health plans"--plans in existence on March 23, 2010 that have not thereafter made specified changes in coverage, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 18011(a), (e); 45 CFR 147.140(g) (2018). "Federal statutes often include exemptions for small employers, and such provisions have never been held to undermine the interests served by these statutes."  <em>Hobby Lobby</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at 763 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, J., dissenting).  "[T]he grandfathering provision," "far from ranking as a categorical exemption, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is temporary, intended to be a means for gradually transitioning employers into mandatory coverage."  <em>Id.</em>, at 764 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"The [Institute of Medicine] is an arm of the National Academy of Sciences, an organization Congress established for the explicit purpose of furnishing advice to the Government."  <em>Id</em>., at 742, n.&nbsp;3 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;HRSA, U.&nbsp;S. Dept. of Health and Human Services (HHS), Women's Preventive Services Guidelines, www.hrsa.gov/womens-guidelines/ index.html.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;HRSA, HHS, Women's Preventive Services Guidelines, <em>supra</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;77 Fed. Reg. 8725 (alterations and internal quotation marks omitted).  Proponents of the Women's Health Amendment specifically anticipated that HRSA would require coverage of family planning services.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, 155 Cong. Rec. 28841 (2009) (statement of Sen. Boxer); <em>id.</em>, at 28843 (statement of Sen. Gillibrand); <em>id.</em>, at 28844 (statement of Sen. Mikulski); <em>id.</em>, at 28869 (statement of Sen. Franken); <em>id</em>., at 28876 (statement of Sen. Cardin); <em>ibid</em>. (statement of Sen. Feinstein); <em>id.</em>, at 29307 (statement of Sen. Murray).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This opinion refers to the contraceptive-coverage accommodation made in 2013 as the "self-certification accommodation."  See <em>ante</em>, at 6 (opinion of the Court).  Although this arrangement "requires the issuer to bear the cost of [contraceptive] services, HHS has determined that th[e] obligation will not impose any net expense on issuers because its cost will be less than or equal to the cost savings resulting from th[ose] services."  <em>Hobby Lobby</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at 698-699.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nonprofit and closely held for-profit organizations with "sincerely held moral<em> </em>convictions" against contraception also qualify for the exemption.  45 CFR 147.133(a)(1)(i), (a)(2).  Unless otherwise noted, this opinion refers to the religious and moral exemptions together as "the exemption" or "the blanket exemption."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This opinion uses "Brief for HHS et&nbsp;al." to refer to the Brief for Petitioners in No. 19-454, filed on behalf of the Departments of HHS, Treasury, and Labor, the Secretaries of those Departments, and the President.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The only language to which the Court points in support of its contrary conclusion is the phrase "as provided for."  See<em> ante</em>, at 15.  This phrase modifies<em> </em>"additional preventive care and screenings."  300gg-13(a)(4).  It therefore speaks to <em>what </em>services shall be provided, not <em>who</em> must provide them.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court does not say whether, in its view, the exemption authority it claims for women's preventive care exists as well for HRSA's children's preventive-care guidelines.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;HRSA, HHS, Organization, www.hrsa.gov/about/organization/ index.html.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A more logical choice would have been HHS's Office for Civil Rights (OCR), which "enforces .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. conscience and religious freedom laws" with respect to HHS programs.  HHS, OCR, About Us, www.hhs.gov/ocr/about-us/index.html.  Indeed, when the Senate introduced an amendment to the ACA similar in character to the blanket exemption, a measure that failed to pass, the Senate instructed that OCR administer the exemption. 158 Cong. Rec. 1415 (2012) (proposed amendment); <em>id</em>., at 2634 (vote tabling amendment).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;On the broad scope the Court today attributes to the "ministerial exception," see <em>Our Lady of Guadalupe School</em> v. <em>Morrissey-Berru</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2020).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Government does not argue that my view of the limited compass of 300gg-13(a)(4) imperils the self-certification accommodation.  Brief for HHS et&nbsp;al. 19-20.  But see <em>ante</em>, at 18, n.&nbsp;9 (opinion of the Court)<em>.  </em>That accommodation aligns with the Court's decisions under the Religious Freedom Restoration Act of 1993 (RFRA).  See <em>infra</em>, at 14-15.  It strikes a balance between women's health and religious opposition to contraception, preserving women's access to seamless, no-cost contraceptive coverage, but imposing the obligation to provide such coverage directly on insurers, rather than on the objecting employer.  See <em>supra</em>, at 6; <em>infra</em>, at 18-20. The blanket exemption, in contrast, entirely disregards women employees' preventive care needs.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;But see, <em>e.g.</em>,<em> </em>Brief for Professors of Criminal Law et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici</em> <em>Curiae</em> 8-11 (RFRA does not grant agencies independent rulemaking authority; instead, laws allegedly violating RFRA must be challenged in court).  No party argues that agencies can act to cure violations of RFRA only after a court has found a RFRA violation, and this opinion does not adopt any such view.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Government notes that 2.9 million people were covered by the 209 plans that previously utilized the self-certification accommodation.  83 Fed. Reg. 57577.  One hundred nine of those plans covering 727,000 people, the Government estimates, will use the religious exemption, while 100 plans covering more than 2.1 million people will continue to use the self-certification accommodation.  <em>Id.</em>, at 57578<em>.  </em>If more plans, or plans covering more people, use the new exemption, more women than the Government estimates will be affected.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Title X "is the only federal grant program dedicated solely to providing individuals with comprehensive family planning and related preventive health services."  HHS, About Title X Grants, www.hhs.gov/opa/ title-x-family-planning/about-title-x-grants/index.html.  A recent rule makes women who lose contraceptive coverage due to the religious exemption eligible for Title X services.  See 84 Fed. Reg. 7734 (2019).  Expanding <em>eligibility</em>, however, "does nothing to ensure Title X providers actually have capacity to meet the expanded client population."  Brief for National Women's Law Center et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>22.  Moreover, that same rule forced 1,041 health providers, serving more than 41% of Title X patients, out of the Title X provider network due to their affiliation with abortion providers.  84 Fed. Reg. 7714; Brief for Planned Parenthood Federation of America et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>18-19.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Remarkably<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>, Justice Alito</small></span> maintains that stripping women of insurance coverage for contraceptive services imposes no burden.  See <em>ante</em>, at 18 (concurring opinion).  He reaches this conclusion because, in his view, federal law does not require the contraceptive coverage denied to women under the exemption.  <em>Ibid</em>.  Congress, however, called upon HRSA to specify contraceptive and other preventive services for women in order to ensure equality in women employees' access to healthcare, thus safeguarding their health and well-being.  See <em>supra,</em> at 2-5. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As above stated, the Government does not defend the moral exemption under RFRA.  See <em>supra</em>, at 13.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> disputes the relevance of <em>Roy</em>, asserting that the religious adherent in that case faced no penalty for noncompliance with the legal requirement under consideration.  See <em>ante</em>, at 6, n.&nbsp;5.  As <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> acknowledges, however, the critical inquiry has two parts.  See <em>ante</em>, at 6-7.  It is not enough to ask whether noncompliance entails "substantial adverse practical consequences."  One must also ask whether compliance substantially burdens religious exercise.  Like <em>Roy</em>, my dissent homes in on the latter question.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even if RFRA sweeps more broadly than the Court's pre-<em>Smith</em> jurisprudence in some respects, see <em>Hobby Lobby</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at 695, n.&nbsp;3; but see <em>id.</em>, at 749-750 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, J., dissenting), there is no cause to believe that Congress jettisoned this fundamental distinction.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> ignores the distinction between (1) a request for an accommodation with regard to one's own conduct, and (2) an attempt to require others to conform their conduct to one's own religious beliefs.  This distinction is fatal to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span>'s argument that the selfcertification accommodation violates RFRA.  See <em>ante</em>, at 6-10.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Religious organizations have observed that, under the self-certification accommodation, insurers need not, and do not, provide contraceptive coverage under a separate policy number.  Supp. Brief for Petitioners in <em>Zubik</em> v. <em>Burwell</em>, O. T. 2015, No. 14-1418, p. 1.  This objection does not relate to a religious employer's own conduct; instead, it concerns the <em>insurer's</em> conduct.  See <em>supra</em>, at 18-19.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> asserts that the Little Sisters' "situation [is] the same as that of the conscientious objector in <em>Thomas</em> [v. <em>Review Bd. of Ind. Employment Security Div</em>., 450 U.&nbsp;S. 707, 715 (1981)]."  <em>Ante</em>, at 9-10.  I disagree<em>.  </em>In <em>Thomas</em>, a Jehovah's Witness objected to "work[ing] on weapons," 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 710, which is what his employer required of him.  As above stated, however, the Little Sisters have no objection to objecting, the only other action the self-certification accommodation requires of them.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Given this conclusion, I need not address whether the exemption is procedurally invalid.  See <em>ante</em>, at 22-26 (opinion of the Court).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the Court does not reach the issue, the District Court did not abuse its discretion in issuing a nationwide injunction.  The Administrative Procedure Act contemplates nationwide relief from invalid agency action.  See 5 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 706(2) (empowering courts to "hold unlawful and set aside agency action").  Moreover, the nationwide reach of the injunction "was 'necessary to provide complete relief to the plaintiffs.'&nbsp;"  <em>Trump</em> v. <em>Hawaii</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___, n.&nbsp;15 (2018) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 25, n.&nbsp;13) (quoting <em>Madsen</em> v. <em>Women's Health Center</em>,<em> Inc</em>., 512 U.&nbsp;S. 753, 765 (1994)).  Harm to Pennsylvania and New Jersey, the Court of Appeals explained, occurs because women who lose benefits under the exemption "will turn to state-funded services for their contraceptive needs and for the unintended pregnancies that may result from the loss of coverage."  930 F.&nbsp;3d, at 562.  This harm is not bounded by state lines. The Court of Appeals noted, for example, that some 800,000 residents of Pennsylvania and New Jersey work--and thus receive their health insurance--out of State.  <em>Id.</em>, at 576.  Similarly, many students who attend colleges and universities in Pennsylvania and New Jersey receive their health insurance from their parents' out-of-state health plans.  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');

  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };

      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];

  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      var toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        var pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if(practiceCode)
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    var nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextsteps form');
    nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(ev) {
      _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
    });

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    var searchComponent = document.querySelector('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    if (searchComponent) {
      searchComponent.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/")
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          else
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
        }
      });
    }

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    var submitButton = document.getElementById('submit-button');
    var heroContentInput = document.querySelector('.hero-content input[type="submit"]');
    var searchSecondaryInput = document.querySelector('.search-secondary input[type="submit"]');
    var submitButtons = [
      submitButton, 
      heroContentInput, 
      searchSecondaryInput
    ];
    for (var i = 0; i < submitButtons.length; i++) {
      var button = submitButtons[i];
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
        }
      });
    }

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      var forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      for (var i = 0; i < forms.length; i++) {
        var form = forms[i];
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
        });
      }
    }

    //CTA
    var links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    for (var i = 0; i < links.length; i++) {
      var link = links[i];
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public').length > 0) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });

    //Right Rail

        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);

    //Browse

      var lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');

          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);

            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {

          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      }
    }

    //data-search-location
    var linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    for (var i = 0; i < linksWithSearchLocation.length; i++) {

        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = $(this).data("search-location") || $(this).parents("[data-search-location]").data("search-location");
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}
